the
<b><font>the</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðiː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ði]</font> <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðə]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ði]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">determiner</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>The</b> is used as the definite article before a noun.<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> used when you are referring to a particular thing or person that has already been mentioned or is already known about<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Have you locked the door?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I have to look after the children.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>A nurse brought me some bread and coffee, but the bread was stale and the coffee tasted of soap.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1a.</b></font> used when it is obvious which one you are referring to because there is only one<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The sun was hidden behind a cloud.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the US president</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’ll meet you outside the main entrance.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1b.</b></font> used when you are referring to familiar things that people deal with regularly<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I looked up at the ceiling.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Suddenly all the lights went out.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>a place to shelter from the wind and the rain</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Usage note </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Use <b>the</b> before words such as ‘school’, ‘university’, and ‘prison’ when you are referring to a particular building: <font color="#052a57"><em>Inspectors will be visiting the school next week.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Do not use <b>the</b> when you are referring to the institution in general: <font color="#052a57"><em>Her husband has been sent to prison for three years.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In American English, however, <b>the</b> is used with ‘hospital’ for referring to the institution in general: <font color="#052a57"><em>She was admitted to the hospital with minor head injuries.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> used before a singular noun when making a general statement about people or things of a particular type<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The average university student is not very interested in politics.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>People have come to depend on the car as their only means of transport.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Usage note </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Do not use <b>the</b> when you are talking about travelling by a particular form of transport: <font color="#052a57"><em>We went by plane.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3.</b></font> used when referring to a part of a particular thing<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Take the top of the piece of paper and fold it so that it is even with the bottom.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3a.</b></font> used when referring to parts of the body<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>He has a gunshot wound in the neck.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>4.</b></font> used before dates or periods of time<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the 4th of July</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The next day he flew back to Paris.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>the 1930s/​the eighties etc</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>popular music of the 1960s</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>5.</b></font> used when explaining which person or thing you are referring to<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Who was the actor who played Romeo?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>We live in the house with green shutters.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>We shall be interviewing Peter Carey, the author.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>6.</b></font> used before a noun that refers to an action, especially when it is followed by ‘of’<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the destruction of a whole city</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the death of Princess Diana</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the burning of several houses</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>7.</b></font> used before the names of seas, rivers, deserts, or groups of mountains<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the Pacific Ocean</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the Thames</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the Sahara</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the Alps</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>7a.</b></font> used before the names of large public buildings<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the Town Hall</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the Science Museum</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://words used in the names of buildings">words used in the names of buildings</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <a href="entry://farm buildings and structures where animals are kept">farm buildings and structures where animals are kept</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Usage note </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>The</b> is not usually used before the names of individual mountains: <font color="#052a57"><em>Mount Everest</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>The</b> is not usually used before the names of streets, towns, countries, states, or continents: <font color="#052a57"><em>My parents live in Kenya.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There are a few exceptions, for example: <font color="#052a57"><em>the Sudan</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>the United States</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>the Hague</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>8.</b></font> used before an adjective to form a noun when you are referring to the type of thing that is described by the adjective<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>the impossible/​supernatural/​unthinkable etc</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Are we attempting to achieve the impossible?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>a belief in the supernatural</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>9.</b></font> used before <i>superlative</i> adjectives and words such as ‘first’, ‘second’, ‘next’, and ‘last’<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the best hotel in Paris</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>It was the first time I’d ever met a real live film star.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>10.</b></font> used before an adjective to form a plural noun when you are referring to people of a particular type or people from a particular country<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>the rich/​poor/​young/​unemployed etc</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>a policy of taxing the rich to help the poor</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>the French/​British/​Japanese etc</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>The Japanese eat a lot of seafood.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>10a.</b></font> used before a plural name when you are referring to members of a particular family<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>She’s been living with the Wilsons for nearly three months.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Usage note </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Do not use <b>the</b> before a person’s name when it is used in the possessive: <font color="#052a57"><em>The car was parked in front of Jim’s house.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>11.</b></font> <font color="orangered">usually in negatives</font> used when saying whether there is enough of something for a particular purpose<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>He didn’t have the strength to go on fighting.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’d like to start my own business – that’s what I’d do if I had the money.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>12.</b></font> used when you are saying what type of musical instrument someone plays<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Lorna plays the violin.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://determiners">determiners</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>13.</b></font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðiː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ði]</font> used when a particular person, thing, or place that you are referring to is the famous one, or is the best or most fashionable<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘The man’s name was Alfred Hitchcock.’ ‘Not the Alfred Hitchcock?’</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>For night-life, Juan-les-Pins is definitely the place.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font be
<b><font>be</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[biː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[bi]</font> <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[bi]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">verb</font> <font color="#af7f7f">[</font><font color="#af7f7f"><i>present tense</i></font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>I</i></font> <font color="#019444">am</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>you/we/they</i></font> <font color="#019444">are</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>he/she/it</i></font> <font color="#019444">is</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>present participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">being</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past tense</i></font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>I/he/she/it</i></font> <font color="#019444">was</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>you/we/they</i></font> <font color="#019444">were</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">been</font><font color="#af7f7f">]</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>Be</b> can have many different forms depending on its subject and on its tense:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;present tense<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I <b>am</b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;he/​she/​it <b>is</b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;we/​you/​they <b>are</b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;past tense<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I/​he/​she/​it <b>was</b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;we/​you/​they <b>were</b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;past participle<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>been</b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;present participle<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>being</b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>Be</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an auxiliary verb in progressive verb tenses or in passive forms (followed by a present participle or a past participle): <font color="#052a57"><em>The train is leaving.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>A window was broken.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a verb (followed by an infinitive with ‘to’): <font color="#052a57"><em>She is to marry Lord Stanhope.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>All books are to be returned by Friday.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>The party was to be a surprise.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a linking verb (followed by an adjective or noun complement): <font color="#052a57"><em>Dad was ill.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>His wife is a doctor.</em></font> (followed by an adverb or preposition): <font color="#052a57"><em>The children are in bed.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Questions and negatives that are formed without ‘do’: <font color="#052a57"><em>Are you busy?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>The book was not expensive.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>Be</b> is often used in question tags: <font color="#052a57"><em>You weren’t listening, were you?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>It’s cold, isn’t it?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Usage note </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In conversation or informal writing <b>am</b> can be shortened to <b>’m</b> , <b>is</b> can be shortened to <b>’s</b> , and <b>are</b> can be shortened to <b>’re</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>I’m so glad you came.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Kay’s still at the office.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>We’re leaving tomorrow.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;These short forms can be followed by <b>not</b> to make negative sentences: <font color="#052a57"><em>I’m not surprised.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>She’s not very strong.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>You’re not helping.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Negative forms can also be shortened: <b>am not</b> can be shortened to <b>aren’t</b> (but only in questions), <b>is not</b> can be shortened to <b>isn’t</b> , <b>are not</b> can be shortened to <b>aren’t</b> , <b>was not</b> can be shortened to <b>wasn’t</b> , and <b>were not</b> can be shortened to <b>weren’t</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>I’m next, aren’t I?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Tom isn’t coming.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>They weren’t very nice.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used in progressive verb tenses</font></b> <font color="#a64728">[auxiliary verb]</font> used for forming the <i>progressive</i> tenses of verbs, that are used for showing actions that are in progress at a particular point in time<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Is everyone listening?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’m studying English Literature.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>We were having breakfast when Terry phoned.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://auxiliary verbs">auxiliary verbs</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used in passive verb forms</font></b> <font color="#a64728">[auxiliary verb]</font> used for forming the passive form of verbs<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Her husband was killed in a car accident.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The orchestra will be conducted by David Norton.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://auxiliary verbs">auxiliary verbs</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used in descriptions</font></b> <font color="#a64728">[linking verb]</font> used for giving information about someone or something, by giving their name, job, position etc, describing them, or saying where they are<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Calvin Schultz is our Marketing Director.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>He wants to be an actor when he leaves school.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>It was a cold frosty morning.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Baltimore is not far from Washington.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Tom’s from a small town in Canada.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Who’s in charge of the Finance Department?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Nancy is tall and very thin.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://linking verbs">linking verbs</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>4.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used for talking about behaviour</font></b> <font color="#a64728">[linking verb]</font> used for saying how someone behaves, or for telling them how to behave<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Be quiet! I can’t hear what they’re saying.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>He’s just being silly again – pay no attention.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://linking verbs">linking verbs</a><sub><font and
<b><font>and</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ænd]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ən]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ənd]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">conjunction</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>And</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a conjunction (joining two words, phrases, or clauses): <font color="#052a57"><em>Rachel plays the piano and sings.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a way of starting a new sentence and relating it to the previous sentence: <font color="#052a57"><em>The telephone isn’t working. And that’s not the only problem.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>‘My name is Chris.’ ‘And my name is Ivan.’</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> used between words, phrases etc in order to connect them together<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the lakes and mountains of Scotland</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Everyone was singing and dancing.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>You cook the lunch, and I’ll look after the children.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://conjunctions">conjunctions</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> used for showing that one thing happens after another<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>He switched off the television and went to bed.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3.</b></font> used for showing what your intention or purpose is after verbs such as ‘go’, ‘come’, ‘try’, or ‘wait’<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’ll try and find out where we can buy tickets.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Come and see our new kitchen.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>4.</b></font> used for showing that one thing causes another<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The government lied to us in the past and now no one will believe them.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>5.</b></font> used for connecting words that are repeated for emphasis<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The situation is getting more and more complicated all the time.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’ve tried and tried, but I can’t persuade him to change his mind.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>It’s going to take years and years to repair all the damage.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>6.</b></font> used in calculations for showing that numbers are added together<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Two and two is four.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://expressions used in calculation and mathematics">expressions used in <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>7.</b></font> <font color="#af7f7f">spoken</font> used for introducing a sentence when you are making an announcement, asking a question, or changing the subject<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>And now, here with the sports news is Kevin Leary.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>And so what have you been doing lately?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>8.</b></font> <font color="#af7f7f">mainly spoken</font> used when you pause to make a numbers after the word <i>‘hundred’</i> or ‘thousand’ when it is followed by words for numbers less than 100<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>a hundred and ten <font>II.</font></font> <font>a</font></b> <sup>2</sup> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[eɪ]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ə]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">determiner</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>A</b> or <b>an</b> is used as an indefinite article , usually followed by a singular countable noun.<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<a href="entry://A">A</a> is used when the next word begins with a consonant.<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;An is used when the next word begins with a vowel sound.<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When a word begins with the letter ‘u’ that is pronounced / <font color="#21887d">juː</font> /, the word is treated as starting with a consonant: <font color="#052a57"><em>a university</em></font> .<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When a word begins with a silent ‘h’, it is treated as starting with a vowel: <font color="#052a57"><em>an hour</em></font> .<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The names of the letters f, h, l, m, n, r, s, and x begin with vowel sounds, so abbreviations that begin with one of these letters are treated as starting with a vowel: <font color="#052a57"><em>an MP</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>an HGV</em></font> .<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> used when you are mentioning a person or a thing for the first time, or when the person listening to you does not already know about them<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I have an idea.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>There’s a to
<b><font>to</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[tuː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[tu]</font> <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[tə, tʊ]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[tə]</font> <font color="#6020ae">adverb, preposition</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>To</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;before the basic form of a verb to form the infinitive: <font color="#052a57"><em>I want to go home.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>It’s nice to see you again.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a preposition (followed by a noun): <font color="#052a57"><em>We drove to Newport.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an adverb (without a following noun): <font color="#052a57"><em>I pulled the door to.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used as part of an infinitive</font></b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1a.</b></font> used for forming an infinitive that is the subject, object, or <i>complement</i> of a verb, or is the complement of an adjective, noun, or pronoun<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I hope to see you next week.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The system is easy to understand.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I didn’t know what to say.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Hattersley’s decision to retire from politics was not completely unexpected.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1b.</b></font> used for showing the purpose of an action<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Nobody came to help me.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I am writing to thank you for your generous gift.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>To call security, just press this button here.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>in order to do <b><font color="#019444">used for showing a possible range</font></b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>18a.</b></font> used when showing the whole range of numbers between and including the two that you mention<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>a toy suitable for children aged three to five</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>from...to</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>the numbers from one to ten</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>18b.</b></font> used when showing how large a range of people or things is<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>from...to</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>I’ve written articles about everything from sexual morality to gardening.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>18c.</b></font> used when you say that a number is within a particular range because you are not sure of the exact number<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Only about 20 to 25 per cent of the population voted for this government.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://inexact and inaccurate">inexact and inaccurate</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">synonym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>19.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">needed for something</font></b> used for showing that something is an important or necessary part of something else<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the answer to an important question</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>the keys to my desk</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>20.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">while there is a sound</font></b> used for saying the particular sound that can be heard at the same time that something happens<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I awoke to the sound of birds singing.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>21.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">touching something</font></b> used for saying what something else is touching or pressing against<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Clasping the child to her breast, she began to weep.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font have
<b><font>have</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[hæv]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[əv]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[həv]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">verb</font> <font color="#af7f7f">[</font><font color="#af7f7f"><i>present tense</i></font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>I/you/we/they</i></font> <font color="#019444">have</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>he/she/it</i></font> <font color="#019444">has</font> {QUALIFIER}strong{/QUALIFIER} <font color="#21887d">[hæz]</font> {QUALIFIER}weak{/QUALIFIER} <font color="#21887d">[əz]</font> <font color="#21887d">[həz]</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>present participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">having</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past tense</i></font> <font color="#019444">had</font> {QUALIFIER}strong{/QUALIFIER} <font color="#21887d">[hæd]</font> {QUALIFIER}weak{/QUALIFIER} <font color="#21887d">[əd]</font> <font color="#21887d">[həd]</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">had</font><font color="#af7f7f">]</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>Have</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an auxiliary verb in perfect tenses of verbs (followed by a past participle): <font color="#052a57"><em>We have lived here for 20 years.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Who’s eaten all the grapes?</em></font> (used without a following past participle): <font color="#052a57"><em>Ellen hasn’t finished, but I have.</em></font> Questions, negatives, and question tags using the auxiliary verb <b>have</b> are formed without <b>do</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>Has the meeting finished?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>You haven’t eaten anything.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>The customers haven’t complained, have they?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a transitive verb used in descriptions and for talking about possession, relationships, or the state that someone or something is in: <font color="#052a57"><em>She has dark curly hair.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>He had two sisters.</em></font> This use of <b>have</b> is never in progressive or passive forms. <b>Have got</b> is often used instead of <b>have</b> for these meanings, especially in spoken English and informal writing: <font color="#052a57"><em>Alan’s got a new bike.</em></font> Questions and negatives can be formed by using <b>have got</b> , <b>have</b> alone, or <b>do</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>Have you got any money?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Have you any money?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Do you have any money?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>We haven’t got any money.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>We haven’t any money.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>We don’t have any money.</em></font> Question tags are formed with <b>have</b> when the main verb is <b>have got</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>They’ve got a lovely garden, haven’t they?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a transitive verb used for talking about actions and experiences: <font color="#052a57"><em>Let’s have a swim before lunch.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>I had a good time at the party.</em></font> This use of <b>have</b> can sometimes be in the progressive but is almost never in the passive: <font color="#052a57"><em>She’s having a baby.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Are you having a drink?</em></font> <b>Have got</b> is not used, and neither short forms of <b>have</b> nor weak forms of pronunciation are ever used in these meanings. Questions, negatives, and question tags are formed with <b>do</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>Did you have a nice walk?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>I didn’t have breakfast this morning.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>They had quite a bad accident, didn’t they?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a transitive verb (followed by an object and then a participle or infinitive without ‘to’): <font color="#052a57"><em>How often do you have your hair cut?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>I’ll have someone clean out your room.</em></font> This use of <b>have</b> can be in the progressive: <font color="#052a57"><em>I’m having all the carpets cleaned.</em></font> Questions, negatives, and question tags are formed with <b>do</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>Did you have the engine checked?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a verb used for talking about what is necessary (followed by a verb in the infinitive with ‘to’): <font color="#052a57"><em>I had to wait for an hour.</em></font> (followed by ‘to’ without a verb in the infinitive): <font color="#052a57"><em>We’ll fight for our rights if we have to.</em></font> This use of <b>have</b> can be in the progressive: <font color="#052a57"><em>I was having to work every weekend.</em></font> <b>Have got to</b> is often used instead of <b>have to</b> , especially in spoken English and in informal writing: <font color="#052a57"><em>You’ve got to show your passport.</em></font> Questions can be formed using <b>do</b> , <b>have got</b> , or <b>have</b> alone: <font color="#052a57"><em>Do we have to pay now?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Have we got to pay now?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Have we to pay now?</em></font> Negative sentences are usually formed with <b>do</b> or <b>have got</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>You don’t have to pay yet.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>You haven’t got to pay yet.</em></font> Question tags are usually formed with <b>do</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>We have to take a test, don’t we?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Usage note </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In conversation or informal writing the auxiliary use of <b>have</b> is often shortened. <b>Have</b> can be shortened to <b>’ve</b> , <b>has</b> to <b>’s</b> , and <b>had</b> to <b>’d</b> : <font color="#052a57"><em>They’ve already left.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>John’s lost his ticket.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>I’d forgotten to tell you.</em></font> These short forms can be followed by ‘not’ to make negative sentences: <font color="#052a57"><em>I’ve not seen anyone.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>She’d not arrived.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The ordinary transitive uses of <b>have</b> are not usually shortened, though <b>’ve</b> and <b>’d</b> forms are sometimes possible: <font color="#052a57"><em>I’ve a sister who lives in York.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Short forms are usually used before ‘got’: <font color="#052a57"><em>I’ve got an idea.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>Jack’s got the tickets.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Negative forms can also be shortened: <b>have not</b> can be shortened to <b>haven’t</b> , <b>has not</b> can be shortened to <b>hasn’t</b> , and <b>had not</b> can be shortened to <b>hadn’t</b> .<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used for forming perfect tenses</font></b> <font color="#a64728">[auxiliary verb]</font> <font color="orangered">never progressive</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1a.</b></font> used for forming the <i>perfect tenses</i> of verbs. The perfect tenses are used for talking about what happened or began before now or another point in time<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Has anybody seen Dave this afternoon?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’ve been looking for you everywhere.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>She hadn’t eaten anything for three days.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘Has Jerry done his homework?’ ‘No, he hasn’t.’</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘Have you washed your hands?’ ‘Of course I have.’</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>We didn’t get a chance to talk to her, but I wish we had.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Young Benson’s done very well, hasn’t he?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>So, you’ve decided to join the party, have you?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font it
<b><font>it</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ɪt]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">pronoun</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>It</b> can be used as the subject, object, or complement of a verb or the object of a preposition.<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">referring to something that has been mentioned</font></b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1a.</b></font> used for referring to a thing, situation, place, animal etc when it has already been mentioned or when it is obvious which one you are referring to<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I can’t find my ticket. color="#052a57"><em>Her dog was howling at the door, so he let it in.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>You should come to Rome – it’s a wonderful city.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>If you’d come along and support us, we’d really appreciate it.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://pronouns">pronouns</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> I
<b><font color="#b904af">▪ <font>I.</font></font> <font>I</font></b> <sup>1</sup> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[aɪ]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">pronoun</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>I</b> is used as the subject of a verb. In very formal English, <b>I</b> can be used after the verb ‘to be’, especially before a relative clause: <font color="#052a57"><em>It is I who will suffer.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;used for referring to yourself when you are the person speaking or writing<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I don’t like cats.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I didn’t hurt you, did I?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Main entry:</font></font> <a href="entry://i">i</a><br><b><font color="#b904af">▪ <font>III.</font></font> I</b> that
<b><font>that</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðæt]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">adverb, conjunction, determiner, pronoun</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>That</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a determiner (followed by a noun): <font color="#052a57"><em>Give me that hammer.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a demonstrative pronoun (without a following noun): <font color="#052a57"><em>Who gave you that?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a conjunction (connecting two clauses): <font color="#052a57"><em>I didn’t know that she was married.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a relative pronoun (forming the subject, object, or complement of a relative clause): <font color="#052a57"><em>It’s a song that my mother taught me.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an adverb (before an adjective or adverb): <font color="#052a57"><em>Three years? I can’t wait that long.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <font color="#af7f7f">[</font><font color="#af7f7f"><i>plural</i></font> <font color="#019444">those</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðəʊz]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðoʊz]</font><font color="#af7f7f">]</font> <b><font color="#019444">the one that is known about</font></b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1a.</b></font> used when you are referring to someone or something that has already been mentioned<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I know there’s a problem, but I haven’t got time to worry about that now.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>No, I’m not in love with Ken. Where did you get that idea?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>It was a secret – that’s why they never talked about it.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Why don’t you ask Carmen? That’s who I’d choose.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1b.</b></font> <font color="#af7f7f">spoken</font> used for referring to someone or something that the person you are speaking to already knows about<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The engine’s started making that noise you are looking at</font></b> <font color="#af7f7f">spoken</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2a.</b></font> used for referring to someone or something that is not very near to you but that you can see or point at<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>That’s Jerry’s car, over there.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font It suits you.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>What’s that you’re holding?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3.</b></font> <font color="#af7f7f">[</font><font color="#af7f7f"><i>plural</i></font> <font color="#019444">those</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðəʊz]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðoʊz]</font><font color="#af7f7f">]</font> <b><font color="#019444">a past time or event</font></b><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3a.</b></font> used for referring to a period, event, or experience in the past<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>There were no telephones in those days.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Remember that time we all went to the lake?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>He once played for England, but that was a long time ago.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3b.</b></font> used for referring to something that has just happened<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>That was fun. We must do it again some time.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>4.</b></font> <font color="#af7f7f">[</font><font color="#af7f7f"><i>plural</i></font> <font color="#019444">those</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font> <font color="#21887d">[ðəʊz]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font> <font color="#21887d">[ðoʊz]</font><font color="#af7f7f">]</font> <b><font color="#019444">a specific one</font></b> used when you are saying which person or thing you are referring to<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>All the details were in that letter I sent you last week.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Those who could not walk were left to die by the roadside.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>that of</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>The voice was that of an elderly woman.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>5.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">the person you are talking to</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">British</font> <font color="#af7f7f">spoken</font> used for asking who someone is when you are telephoning them<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Hello, is that Robert Hoffman? <b><font>you</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[juː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ju]</font> <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[jə]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[jʊ]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">determiner, pronoun</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>You</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a subject or object pronoun : <font color="#052a57"><em>What do you want?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>I like you.</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>I’ll make the tea for you.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a determiner : <font color="#052a57"><em>You children had better go to bed now.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> used for referring to the person or people that you are talking or writing to<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Do you like oranges?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I can’t really trust you, can I?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Is there anything I can do to show you how sorry I am?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I want to go to my room, if you don’t mind.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’ll give it to you if you want it.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Tonight you men can wash the dishes.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I didn’t see you girls sitting there.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> used for referring to people in general<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>He wasn’t very good-looking, but you could tell he thought he was.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>When you’re with him, he makes you feel very important.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>If you exercise every day, you’ll feel a lot better.</em></font>

he
<b><font color="#b904af">▪ <font>I.</font></font> <font>he</font></b> <sup>1</sup> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[hiː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[hi]</font> <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[i]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[hi]</font> <font color="#6020ae">pronoun</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>He</b> is used as the subject of a verb. Some people prefer to use <b>he</b> after the verb ‘to be’ but this sounds very formal and old-fashioned: <font color="#052a57"><em>It is he whom I love.</em></font> In American English, however, ‘This is he’ is the usual way that a man replies on the telephone when someone asks to speak to him: <font color="#052a57"><em>‘May I speak with John?’ ‘This is he.’</em></font> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Words that may cause offence </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;He , him , his , and himself are sometimes used for referring to a person of either sex, for example in writing about something that may apply to a man or to a woman. However, many people think that this use suggests that women are not included or that men are more important than women. To avoid causing offence, you can use expressions such as he or she , he/​she , s/​he , him/​her , or him/​herself in writing. In conversation, many people say they , them , their , and themselves to refer to a person without mentioning whether the person is male or female. <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> used for referring to a man, boy, or male animal when they have already been mentioned or when it is obvious which one you are referring to<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I told William, but he didn’t believe me.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Like all dogs, he’ll chase a rabbit if he sees one.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://personal pronouns">personal pronouns</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> <font color="#af7f7f">old-fashioned</font> used in a general way for referring to any person whether they are male or female<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Everyone has a right to say what he thinks.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://personal pronouns">personal pronouns</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3.</b></font> <b>He</b><font do
<b><font color="#b904af">▪ <font>I.</font></font> <font>do</font></b> <sup>1</sup> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[duː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[du]</font> <font color="#6020ae">verb</font> <font color="#af7f7f">[</font><font color="#af7f7f"><i>present tense</i></font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>I/you/we/they</i></font> <font color="#019444">do</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>he/she/it</i></font> <font color="#019444">does</font> {QUALIFIER}strong{/QUALIFIER} <font color="#21887d">[dʌz]</font> {QUALIFIER}weak{/QUALIFIER} <font color="#21887d">[dəz]</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>present participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">doing</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past tense</i></font> <font color="#019444">did</font> <font color="#21887d">[dɪd]</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">done</font> <font color="#21887d">[dʌn]</font><font color="#af7f7f">]</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>Do</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an auxiliary verb , especially for forming questions and negatives in the simple present or simple past tense (followed by an infinitive without ‘to’): <font color="#052a57"><em>Did you enjoy the party?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>She does not understand.</em></font> In conversation and informal written English the following negative short forms of the auxiliary verb are used: <b>doesn’t</b> , <b>don’t</b> , and <b>didn’t</b> .<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an intransitive verb that replaces or refers to an ordinary verb that was in a previous clause or sentence: <font color="#052a57"><em>You know as much as I do</em></font> <font color="#052a57"><em>(=as much as I know)</em></font> <font color="#052a57"><em>.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an auxiliary verb to form a question tag in the simple present or past tense (when there is no auxiliary or modal verb in the main clause): <font color="#052a57"><em>You teach English, don’t you?</em></font> (where <b>do</b> is used in the main clause): <font color="#052a57"><em>They don’t believe us, do they?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>He did well, didn’t he?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an ordinary transitive verb : <font color="#052a57"><em>He always did his duty.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an ordinary intransitive verb : <font color="#052a57"><em>I need something sharp. That screwdriver will do.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When <b>do</b> is an ordinary transitive or intransitive verb, questions and negatives are formed by using the auxiliary verb <b>do</b> with it: <font color="#052a57"><em>What do you do in your spare time?</em></font> <font color="#019444">♦</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>He didn’t do his homework.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used for forming a question or negative</font></b> <font color="#a64728">[auxiliary verb]</font> used before another verb for forming a question or a negative<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Do you drive?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>The Queen does not interfere in politics.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>What did the doctor say?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Didn’t they tell you I was coming?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Max doesn’t live here any more.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Don’t believe what he tells you.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://auxiliary verbs">auxiliary verbs</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1a.</b></font> used in a <i>question tag</i> at the end of a sentence when you are asking someone to agree with you, or when you are asking for information<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Young William played well, didn’t he?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>You didn’t see my keys lying around anywhere, did you?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://auxiliary verbs">auxiliary verbs</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used for referring back to a previous verb</font></b> <font color="#a64728">[intransitive]</font> used instead of repeating the same verb that was used earlier in the sentence, or in a previous sentence<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘You promised to come with me.’ ‘No I didn’t.’</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>She doesn’t travel around as much as I do.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I like Chinese food, but George doesn’t.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘I enjoyed our trip to Brighton.’ ‘So did <b><font color="#b904af">▪ <font>I.</font></font> <font>say</font></b> <sup>1</sup> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[seɪ]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">verb</font> <font color="#af7f7f">[</font><font color="#af7f7f"><i>present tense</i></font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>I/you/we/they</i></font> <font color="#019444">say</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>he/she/it</i></font> <font color="#019444">says</font> <font color="#21887d">[sez]</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>present participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">saying</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past tense</i></font> <font color="#019444">said</font> <font color="#21887d">[sed]</font> <font color="#af7f7f"><i>past participle</i></font> <font color="#019444">said</font><font color="#af7f7f">]</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Get it right: say </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Unlike the verb <b>tell </b> , the verb <b>say </b> is never used with a personal object. If you want to refer to a personal object after <b>say </b> , use the preposition <b>to </b> :<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font>✗</font><font color="#052a57"><em> Perhaps people misunderstand what I want to </em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>say them</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>in English.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="green">✓</font><font color="#052a57"><em> Perhaps people misunderstand what I want to </em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>say to them</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>in English.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font>✗</font><font color="#052a57"><em> He wanted to be examined by a civilian doctor after an army doctor had </em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>said him that</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>it was serious.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="green">✓</font><font color="#052a57"><em> He wanted to be examined by a civilian doctor after an army doctor had </em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>said to him that</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>it was serious.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The object of the verb <b>say </b> is usually direct speech or a <b>that </b> -clause which reports what someone has said:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘That’s not true!’</em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>she said</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>, but her voice betrayed her.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Climate experts</em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>say that</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>by 2100 rainfall levels in some areas may rise to five times what they are today.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Don’t use <b>tell </b> in structures like this:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font>✗</font><font color="#052a57"><em> He </em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>told that</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>the Japanese representatives tend to be less confident about speaking English.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="green">✓</font><font color="#052a57"><em> He </em></font> <b><font color="#052a57"><em>said that</em></font> </b> <font color="#052a57"><em>the Japanese representatives tend to be less confident about speaking English.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;➡  <a href="entry://tell">tell</a> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <font color="#a64728">[intransitive/transitive]</font> to express something using words<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘Pleased to meet you,’ he said with a smile.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font this
<b><font>this</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðɪs]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">adverb, determiner, pronoun</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>This</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a determiner (followed by a noun): <font color="#052a57"><em>He gave me this diamond ring.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a demonstrative pronoun (without a following noun): <font color="#052a57"><em>This is the photograph you asked for.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as an adverb (before an adjective, adverb, or ‘much’): <font color="#052a57"><em>It’s a long time since I felt this they
<b><font>they</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[ðeɪ]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">pronoun</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>They</b> is used as the subject of a verb: <font color="#052a57"><em>They killed him.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In formal English <b>they</b> can also be used after the verb ‘to be’, especially before a relative clause: <font color="#052a57"><em>It is they who are telling lies.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Usage note </font></i></font>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In spoken English, <b>they</b> , <b>them</b> , <b>their</b> , and <b>themselves</b> are used by many people for referring to a person without mentioning whether the person is male or female, especially when referring back to a pronoun such as ‘everyone’ or ‘someone’: <font color="#052a57"><em>What happens if someone changes their mind?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In more formal English, ‘he or she’, ‘him or her’, or ‘himself or herself’ are used instead.<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> used for referring to a group of people or things that have already been mentioned or that are already known about<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I phoned her parents because I knew they were worried.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>It’s hard to choose. They’re all very nice.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://personal pronouns">personal pronouns</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> used instead of ‘he or she’, especially when you are referring back to a word such as ‘everyone’, ‘someone’, or ‘anyone’<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>We should give everyone a chance to say what they think.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://personal pronouns">personal pronouns</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3.</b></font> used for referring to people in general<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<b>they say/​call/​think etc</b><font color="#019444">:</font> <font color="#052a57"><em>They used to call her ‘the Iron Lady’.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://personal pronouns">personal pronouns</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>4.</b></font> used for referring to a government, an organization, or a group of people in authority<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>They’re going to ban smoking in public places.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://personal pronouns">personal pronouns</a><sub><font color="#af7f7f">hyponym</font></sub> <br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>See also:</font></font> <a href="entry://he">he</a> <font color="#019444">▪</font> <a href="entry://them">them</a>
</>

but
<b><font color="#b904af">▪ <font>I.</font></font> <font>but</font></b> <sup>1</sup> <font color="#ff5400">strong</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[bʌt]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#ff5400">weak</font> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[bət]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#6020ae">adverb, conjunction, preposition</font> <font color="#ff5400">★★★</font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><i><font>Summary</font></i></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <b>But</b> can be used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As a conjunction (connecting two phrases or clauses): <font color="#052a57"><em>She’s 83 but she still goes swimming every day.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As a way of starting a new sentence and connecting it to the previous sentence: <font color="#052a57"><em>It was in Cairo that he met Nadia. But that’s another story.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As a preposition (followed by a noun): <font color="#052a57"><em>There’s been nothing but trouble since he came.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As an adverb : <font color="#052a57"><em>We can but hope that things will improve.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used for introducing a different idea</font></b> used for joining two ideas or statements when the second one is different from the first one, or seems surprising after the first one<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>We’re making good progress, but we’ve still got a long way to go.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Anna’s a highly intelligent girl, but she’s rather lazy.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>a simple but effective way of filtering water</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>What we are about to do is perfectly legal. But please don’t discuss it with anyone.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>1a.</b></font> used after a negative for introducing what is true instead<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>His death was not a tragedy, but a release from pain and suffering.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>2.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used for changing the subject</font></b> <font color="#af7f7f">mainly spoken</font> used when you are saying that you should not talk any more about a subject that you have just mentioned<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>It was a very complicated operation. But I don’t want to bore you with all the details.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>There’s also the question of how we’re going to pay for this, but we can discuss that some other time.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>3.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used in polite questions, requests, and statements</font></b> <font color="#af7f7f">spoken</font> used after expressions such as ‘I’m sorry’ and ‘excuse me’ to introduce a polite question, request, or statement<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Excuse me, but is there a post office around here?</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I’m sorry, but all our operators are busy at the moment.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>4.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">except</font></b> used especially after words such as ‘nothing’, ‘everyone’, or ‘anything’ to mean ‘except’<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>She does nothing but grumble all day long.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font to scrap the whole plan and start again.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#019444"><font>Thesaurus:</font></font> <a href="entry://only, except and not <font color="#af7f7f">formal</font> only<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>We can but guess at the extent of the problem.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>Several Japanese companies already operate in the UK – Nissan, Honda, Fujitsu, to name but a few.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#b904af"><b>6.</b></font> <b><font color="#019444">used for expressing surprise</font></b> <font color="#af7f7f">spoken</font> used when you are replying to someone and expressing surprise, disagreement, or <i>annoyance</i><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>‘I’ve decided to resign.’ ‘But why?’</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>But you promised not to do anything without asking me first.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font happen without something else happening or being true<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<font color="#052a57"><em>I never take a bath but the phone rings or someone knocks at the we
<b><font>we</font></b> <font color="#ff5400">UK</font>  <font color="#21887d">[wiː]</font> <font color="#ff5400">US</font>  <font color="#21887d">[wi]</font> <font color="#6020ae">determiner, used in the following ways:<br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a subject pronoun : <font color="#052a57"><em>We help each other.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In very formal English <b>we</b> can be used after the verb ‘to be’, especially before a relative clause: <font color="#052a57"><em>It is we who should be grateful.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;as a determiner : <font color="#052a57"><em>We men will get the fire started.</em></font><br>&nbsp;&nbsp;<font
